<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Hopi" title="Hopi">Hopi</a> girl with "squash blossom" hairdo (<a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Curtis" title="Edward S. Curtis">Edward S. Curtis</a>, 1922)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:194px;max-width:194px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:220px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dama_de_Elche_(M.A.N._Madrid)_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="975" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg/192px-Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg/288px-Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg/384px-Dama_de_Elche_%28M.A.N._Madrid%29_01.jpg 2x" width="192"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Lady_of_Elche" title="Lady of Elche">Lady of Elche</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Leia's unique hairdo in 1977's <i>A New Hope</i> has come to be known as the "<a href="/wiki/Doughnut" title="Doughnut">doughnut</a>" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinnamon_bun" title="Cinnamon bun">cinnamon buns</a>" hairstyle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merlock_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merlock-94">[94]</a></sup> and is iconic of the character and series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Film.2BTV_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Film.2BTV-73">[73]</a></sup> A February 1978 cover story for the British teen magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Jackie_(magazine)" title="Jackie (magazine)">Jackie</a></i> included step-by-step instructions on how to replicate Leia's hair buns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SW_Covers_Jackie_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-SW_Covers_Jackie-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackie_1978_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackie_1978-96">[96]</a></sup> In the 1978 short film parody <i><a href="/wiki/Hardware_Wars" title="Hardware Wars">Hardware Wars</a></i>, Princess Anne-Droid has actual cinnamon buns on the side of her head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miss_Piggy" title="Miss Piggy">Miss Piggy</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Muppet_Show" title="The Muppet Show">The Muppet Show</a></i> copied the hairdo in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(The_Muppet_Show)" title="Star Wars (The Muppet Show)"><i>Star Wars</i>-themed episode</a> of the series in February 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In the 1987 <a href="/wiki/Mel_Brooks" title="Mel Brooks">Mel Brooks</a> comedy film <i><a href="/wiki/Spaceballs" title="Spaceballs">Spaceballs</a></i>, Princess Vespa (<a href="/wiki/Daphne_Zuniga" title="Daphne Zuniga">Daphne Zuniga</a>) appears to have the hairstyle, which is soon revealed to in fact be a large pair of headphones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Film.2BTV_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Film.2BTV-73">[73]</a></sup> In the parody film <i><a href="/wiki/Thumb_Wars" title="Thumb Wars">Thumb Wars</a></i>, the role of Leia was filled by a character named Princess Bunhead, who has two cinnamon rolls for hair. In 2015, Fisher's daughter <a href="/wiki/Billie_Lourd" title="Billie Lourd">Billie Lourd</a>'s character in the horror-comedy TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Scream_Queens_(2015_TV_series)" title="Scream Queens (2015 TV series)">Scream Queens</a></i>, a rich and disaffected sorority girl known as Chanel No. 3, wears earmuffs in every scene as an homage to Fisher's iconic Leia hairstyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Lourd also has a cameo in <i>The Force Awakens</i> (2015) in which she wears Leia's distinctive hair buns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Asked about his inspiration for the hairstyle, <a href="/wiki/George_Lucas" title="George Lucas">George Lucas</a> told <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> in 2008, "In the 1977 film, I was working very hard to create something different that wasn't fashion, so I went with a kind of Southwestern <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Villa" title="Pancho Villa">Pancho Villa</a> woman revolutionary look, which is what that is. The buns are basically from turn-of-the-century Mexico."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> However, in 2015 Linda Rodriguez McRobbie of <i><a href="/wiki/Mental_Floss" title="Mental Floss">Mental Floss</a></i> recounted research suggesting that while female Mexican revolutionaries of that time did not have such elaborate hairstyles, young marriageable <a href="/wiki/Hopi" title="Hopi">Hopi</a> women did, and their "squash blossom whorls" superficially resemble Leia's hair buns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mental_Floss_buns_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mental_Floss_buns-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilemann_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heilemann-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> McRobbie also relates Michael Heilemann's theory that Leia's hairstyle was likely also inspired by that of two earlier characters: Queen Fria in the 1939 <i><a href="/wiki/Flash_Gordon" title="Flash Gordon">Flash Gordon</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">comic strip</a> "The Ice Kingdom of Mongo", and scientist <a href="/wiki/Barnes_Wallis" title="Barnes Wallis">Barnes Wallis</a>’ wife Molly (played by <a href="/wiki/Ursula_Jeans" title="Ursula Jeans">Ursula Jeans</a>) in the 1955 war film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dam_Busters_(film)" title="The Dam Busters (film)">The Dam Busters</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mental_Floss_buns_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mental_Floss_buns-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilemann_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heilemann-106">[106]</a></sup> Lucas was influenced by the <i>Flash Gordon</i> serials, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yavin" title="Battle of Yavin">Battle of Yavin</a> in <i>Star Wars</i> is an <a href="/wiki/Homage_(arts)" title="Homage (arts)">homage</a> to the penultimate bombing sequence in <i>The Dam Busters</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tech_Times_2015_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tech_Times_2015-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph_2015_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_2015-110">[110]</a></sup> Comparisons have also been made to the 4th century BC <a href="/wiki/Iberian_sculpture" title="Iberian sculpture">Iberian sculpture</a> <a href="/wiki/Lady_of_Elche" title="Lady of Elche">Lady of Elche</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mental_Floss_buns_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mental_Floss_buns-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heilemann_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Heilemann-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> as well as the 1920s "earphones" hairstyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mental_Floss_buns_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mental_Floss_buns-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_analysis">Feminist analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Princess_Leia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Feminist analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Leia has been the subject of <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminist</a> analysis. Mark Edlitz wrote for <i>The Huffington Post</i> in 2010 that "Leia is an exemplary personification of female empowerment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edlitz_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edlitz-15">[15]</a></sup> David Bushman, television curator at the <a href="/wiki/Paley_Center_for_Media" title="Paley Center for Media">Paley Center for Media</a>, said in 2012, "From the male perspective ... Princess Leia was a very creditable character for her time—not perfect, but certainly defiant, assertive, and strong."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Schou_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Schou-68">[68]</a></sup> Alyssa Rosenberg of <i>The Washington Post</i> wrote in 2015, "Leia wasn't just the first great heroine of science fiction and fantasy to capture my imagination. She was one of the first characters I encountered whose power came from her political conviction and acumen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Icon_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Icon-16">[16]</a></sup> In her 2007 article "Feminism and the Force: Empowerment and Disillusionment in a Galaxy Far, Far Away", Diana Dominguez cited Leia as a welcome change from the previously uninspiring portrayals of women in film and TV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merlock_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merlock-94">[94]</a></sup> She wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Here was a woman who could play like and with the boys, but who didn't have to become one of the boys and who could, if and when she wanted to, show she liked the boys, a woman who is outspoken, unashamed, and, most importantly, unpunished for being so. She isn't a flirty sex-pot, tossing her hair around seductively to distract the enemy ... She doesn't play the role of "Maternal caretaker", although she does display caring and compassion, or "the sweet innocent damsel" who stands passively by while the men do all the work, but does step aside to let them do what they're good at when it is wise to do so ... Leia is a hero without losing her gendered status; she does not have to play the cute, helpless sex kitten or become sexless and androgynous to get what she wants. She can be strong, sassy, outspoken, bossy, and bitchy, and still be respected and seen as feminine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merlock_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Merlock-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Rosenberg writes that, though at first Luke is an apolitical innocent in search of adventure and Han is a detached opportunist in search of money, both are "influenced by Leia’s passion [and] take their places as full participants in the Rebellion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Icon_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Icon-16">[16]</a></sup> She notes, "Everyone else eventually comes around to Leia’s view of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Icon_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Icon-16">[16]</a></sup> Leia herself, singularly dedicated to her political movement against the Empire, "finds a partner in Han, acknowledging that personal happiness can help her sustain her commitment to building a better galactic order".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Icon_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Icon-16">[16]</a></sup> Rosenberg cites "Leia's willingness to see the best in him, and Han's desire to live up to her belief in him" as a foundation of their relationship, also pointing out his attempts to make her recognize that she has needs like anyone else and should acknowledge that she needs him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Icon_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Icon-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In their 2012 essay "Lightsabers, Political Arenas, and Marriages", Ray Merlock and Kathy Merlock Jackson cite Leia as the successor of earlier science fiction heroines <a href="/wiki/Wilma_Deering" title="Wilma Deering">Wilma Deering</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Buck_Rogers" title="Buck Rogers">Buck Rogers</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Dale_Arden" title="Dale Arden">Dale Arden</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Flash_Gordon" title="Flash Gordon">Flash Gordon</a></i>, and the embodiment of "a new stage in the ongoing presentation of the fairy-tale princess in jeopardy". Writing that "after Leia, no longer would princesses be passive and salvaged simply with a kiss," they note the reflection of the character in later <a href="/wiki/Disney_Princess" title="Disney Princess">Disney Princess</a> animated films and in woman warriors such as <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Ripley" title="Ellen Ripley">Ellen Ripley</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Alien_(franchise)" title="Alien (franchise)"><i>Alien</i> franchise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xena" title="Xena">Xena</a> of the adventure TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Xena:_Warrior_Princess" title="Xena: Warrior Princess">Xena: Warrior Princess</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merlock_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-Merlock-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/A._O._Scott" title="A. O. Scott">A. O. Scott</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> described Leia as "a foremother of <a href="/wiki/Hermione_Granger" title="Hermione Granger">Hermione Granger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katniss_Everdeen" title="Katniss Everdeen">Katniss Everdeen</a> and of countless latter-day Disney princesses. She also foretold the recent, somewhat belated feminist turn in the <i>Star Wars</i> cycle itself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scott20161228_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-scott20161228-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fisher herself described Leia as a "huge" feminist icon, dismissing the suggestion that the character was ever a "damsel in distress". Fisher said of Leia, "She bossed them around. I don't know what your idea of distress is, but that wasn't it! And I wasn't some babe running through the galaxy with my tits bouncing around. So I wasn't threatening to women",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RS_Strike_2015_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-RS_Strike_2015-115">[115]</a></sup> adding "I <i>like</i> Princess Leia. I like how she was feisty. I like how she killed Jabba the Hutt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scott20161228_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-scott20161228-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_bikini">Metal bikini</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Princess_Leia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Metal bikini">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Princess_Leia%27s_bikini" title="Princess Leia's bikini">Princess Leia's bikini</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="533" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg/220px-Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg/330px-Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg/440px-Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Olivia_Munn_Leia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Actress <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Munn" title="Olivia Munn">Olivia Munn</a> cosplaying in the iconic Princess Leia "metal bikini" slave outfit from <i>Return of the Jedi</i> (1983)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Princess_Leia%27s_bikini" title="Princess Leia's bikini">Leia's slave costume</a> when she is held captive by <a href="/wiki/Jabba_the_Hutt" title="Jabba the Hutt">Jabba the Hutt</a> at the beginning of <i>Return of the Jedi</i>—made of brass and dubbed Leia's "Metal Bikini" or "Gold Bikini"—immediately made the character (and Fisher) a "generational <a href="/wiki/Sex_symbol" title="Sex symbol">sex symbol</a>" celebrated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pin-up" title="Pin-up">pin-up</a> posters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Schou_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Schou-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merlock_79_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merlock_79-116">[116]</a></sup> and later merchandising and <a href="/wiki/Cosplay" title="Cosplay">cosplay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Bikini_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Bikini-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blastr_Merch_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blastr_Merch-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWCA_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-SWCA-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Townsend_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Townsend-117">[117]</a></sup> The outfit has gained a cult following of its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Townsend_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Townsend-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rosenberg noted that "the costume has become culturally iconic in a way that has slipped loose from the context of the scenes in which Leia wore it and the things she does after she is forced into the outfit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Bikini_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Bikini-69">[69]</a></sup> Philip Chien of the website <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(website)" title="Wired (website)">Wired</a></i> wrote in 2006, "There's no doubt that the sight of Carrie Fisher in the gold sci-fi swimsuit was burned into the sweaty subconscious of a generation of fanboys hitting puberty in the spring of 1983."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cult_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-cult-118">[118]</a></sup> Acknowledging the opinion of some that the "Slave Leia" iconography tarnishes the character's position as "feminist hero",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Bikini_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Bikini-69">[69]</a></sup> Rosenberg argues:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Leia may be captive in these scenes, but she’s not exactly a compliant fantasy. Instead, she’s biding her time for the moment when she can put that fury into action, carrying out a carefully laid plan to rescue her lover. And when that moment comes, the bikini doesn’t condemn Leia to passivity. She rises, and uses the very chains that bind her to strangle the creature who tried to take away her power by turning her into a sex object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP_Bikini_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-WP_Bikini-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Science fiction filmmaker Letia Clouston concurs, saying "Sci-fi has had a long history of strong female characters. Yes, Princess Leia was in a gold bikini, but she was also the one who single-handedly killed Jabba. When you take into account movies and TV shows like <i><a href="/wiki/Terminator_(franchise)" title="Terminator (franchise)">Terminator</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alien_(franchise)" title="Alien (franchise)">Aliens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Battlestar_Galactica" title="Battlestar Galactica">Battlestar Galactica</a></i>, and even video games like <i><a href="/wiki/Metroid" title="Metroid">Metroid</a></i>, you can see sci-fi has consistently promoted the strength of women more than any other genre."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Schou_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Schou-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_tree">Family tree</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Princess_Leia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Family tree">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
